  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_634c288a-3c7d-4d99-840f-709a3c2793d6'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer7707215650847166090">When Rose Campbell, a shy orphan, arrives at "The Aunt Hill" to live with her six aunts and seven boisterous male cousins, she is quite overwhelmed. How could such a delicate young lady, used to the quiet hallways of a girls' boarding school, exist in such a spirited home? It is the arrival of Uncle Alec that changes everything. Much to the horror of her aunts, Rose's forw</span>
  <span id="freeText7707215650847166090" style="display:none">When Rose Campbell, a shy orphan, arrives at "The Aunt Hill" to live with her six aunts and seven boisterous male cousins, she is quite overwhelmed. How could such a delicate young lady, used to the quiet hallways of a girls' boarding school, exist in such a spirited home? It is the arrival of Uncle Alec that changes everything. Much to the horror of her aunts, Rose's forward-thinking uncle insists that the child get out of the parlor and into the sunshine. And with a little courage and lots of adventures with her mischievous but loving cousins, Rose begins to bloom.<br /><br />Written by the beloved author of <i>Little Women</i>, <i>Eight Cousins</i> is a masterpiece of children's literature. This endearing novel offers readers of all ages an inspiring story about growing up, making friends, and facing life with strength and kindness.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/577601675" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5211606-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505571524p2/5211606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/577601675?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/5211606-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5211606-julie?shelf=massachusetts">massachusetts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer577601675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9000677604644082203">This went from delightful to tedious in thirty pages. It's amazing to me that Jane Austen, who wrote some one hundred years before Alcott, could feel so incredibly modern and this novel so insufferably outdated.<br/><br/>If you like regular lines such as "Oh, you little dear!" and scenes of the older man holding the chin of the thirteen-year-old girl and tenderly kissing her rose-bud lips and telling her to mind him and all of her dreams shall come true (did I mention it is her uncle--her dear, dear uncle</span>
  <span id="freeText9000677604644082203" style="display:none">This went from delightful to tedious in thirty pages. It's amazing to me that Jane Austen, who wrote some one hundred years before Alcott, could feel so incredibly modern and this novel so insufferably outdated.<br /><br />If you like regular lines such as "Oh, you little dear!" and scenes of the older man holding the chin of the thirteen-year-old girl and tenderly kissing her rose-bud lips and telling her to mind him and all of her dreams shall come true (did I mention it is her uncle--her dear, dear uncle?), then by all means, rip into this "classic," and, while you're at it, read Rebecca of Sunnybrook Farm, too!<br /><br />I actually couldn't even make it to the end, but I think she must marry the uncle. I honestly could not suffer through one more scene of the almost 14-year-old cuddling in dear uncle's lap.<br /><br />If someone misled you and you thought this was a brilliant classic. . . go read Anne of Green Gables or Jane Austen or anything Bronte instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9000677604644082203" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10410263148903732563">The latest book in my Louisa May Alcott kick...and I found it generally charming. I love the idea of "throwing out the window" the general practices at the time (wearing tight corsets and belts, taking strong coffees and cordials to improve health, teaching girls to act like 'ladies' instead of allowing them fresh air and exercise) and enjoyed watching young Rose become a picture of health and happiness.   <span id="freeText10410263148903732563" style="display:none">The latest book in my Louisa May Alcott kick...and I found it generally charming. I love the idea of "throwing out the window" the general practices at the time (wearing tight corsets and belts, taking strong coffees and cordials to improve health, teaching girls to act like 'ladies' instead of allowing them fresh air and exercise) and enjoyed watching young Rose become a picture of health and happiness. I also loved the idea that her uncle taught her to be a self-reliant woman (hence the emphasis on housekeeping, cooking, etc.) instead of relying on servants and maids. Some of the ideas were still a bit old-fashioned, but I didn't find it offensive and would definitely encourage my children (daughters and sons) to read this book.</span>

<div id="review_2430203994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2430203994" />
    <a title="Ivonne Rovira" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3803671-ivonne-rovira"><img alt="Ivonne Rovira" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339470651p2/3803671.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2430203994?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivonne Rovira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivonne Rovira" href="/user/show/3803671-ivonne-rovira">Ivonne Rovira</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6640780-eight-cousins">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Alcott devotees only</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2430203994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16600729041062001715">I never knew that Louisa May Alcott ever wrote anything other than <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1934.Little_Women__Little_Women___1_' title='Little Women (Little Women, #1) by Louisa May Alcott' rel='nofollow noopener'>Little Women</a>, its sequels and some scary stories. But in 1875, Alcott published <i>Eight Cousins</i>, a predictable, bathetic novel featuring a ridiculously plucky orphan named Rose Campbell and her seven boy cousins; except for Mac, all of them would make Pollyanna appear a spoiled, selfish misanthrope. It’s no <i>Little Women</i> by a long shot.<br/><br/>The story began well enough, with Rose mourning the death of her beloved invalid father. Her uncle</span>
  <span id="freeText16600729041062001715" style="display:none">I never knew that Louisa May Alcott ever wrote anything other than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1934.Little_Women__Little_Women___1_" title="Little Women (Little Women, #1) by Louisa May Alcott" rel="nofollow noopener">Little Women</a>, its sequels and some scary stories. But in 1875, Alcott published <i>Eight Cousins</i>, a predictable, bathetic novel featuring a ridiculously plucky orphan named Rose Campbell and her seven boy cousins; except for Mac, all of them would make Pollyanna appear a spoiled, selfish misanthrope. It’s no <i>Little Women</i> by a long shot.<br /><br />The story began well enough, with Rose mourning the death of her beloved invalid father. Her uncle, Dr. Alec Campbell, returns from abroad, having lived in China, India and elsewhere in Asia, and becomes her guardian. And that’s when the book rapidly becomes cloying with perfect children who are ever cheerful, helpful and grateful. It was so saccharine that my teeth hurt.<br /><br />The book has one saving grace: It explores the forward-thinking educational methods promoted by Alcott’s father, education reformer Bronson Alcott, including learning by doing, literary discussions, cross-curricular education, and a rejection of rote learning and corporal punishment. These and some other of his methods are now standard in education.<br /><br />Some books can survive through the ages and entertain children (and adults, too) decades after they’re first released: <i>Little Women</i> (1868), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/347151.Understood_Betsy" title="Understood Betsy by Dorothy Canfield Fisher" rel="nofollow noopener">Understood Betsy</a> (1917), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1499952.Daddy_Long_Legs__Daddy_Long_Legs___1_" title="Daddy-Long-Legs (Daddy-Long-Legs, #1) by Jean Webster" rel="nofollow noopener">Daddy-Long-Legs</a> (1912), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/164531.The_Railway_Children" title="The Railway Children by E. Nesbit" rel="nofollow noopener">The Railway Children</a> (1905), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17529.Rebecca_of_Sunnybrook_Farm" title="Rebecca of Sunnybrook Farm by Kate Douglas Wiggin" rel="nofollow noopener">Rebecca of Sunnybrook Farm</a> (1903) or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8127.Anne_of_Green_Gables__Anne_of_Green_Gables___1_" title="Anne of Green Gables (Anne of Green Gables, #1) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne of Green Gables</a> (1908); however, <i>Eight Cousins</i> isn’t one of them. For Alcott super-fans only.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16600729041062001715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1925059474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1925059474" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1925059474?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20558611-eight-cousins">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=theclassics">theclassics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1925059474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5976523110314793856">I love Little Women, I really do. Though I realize in retrospect that a lot of it is sort of . . . preaching the philosophies of the May/March parents. Which is fine, because wanting girls to be strong and self-sufficient is a wonderful thing.<br/><br/>But if you thought Marmee was a little too full of wholesome advice, this is NOT the book for you. The entire book revolves around orphaned Rose, and how her Uncle Alec, a free-thinking doctor, rehabilitates her. The virtues of fresh air, exercise, wholesom</span>
  <span id="freeText5976523110314793856" style="display:none">I love Little Women, I really do. Though I realize in retrospect that a lot of it is sort of . . . preaching the philosophies of the May/March parents. Which is fine, because wanting girls to be strong and self-sufficient is a wonderful thing.<br /><br />But if you thought Marmee was a little too full of wholesome advice, this is NOT the book for you. The entire book revolves around orphaned Rose, and how her Uncle Alec, a free-thinking doctor, rehabilitates her. The virtues of fresh air, exercise, wholesome food, and hard work are touted in every chapter. The evils of corsets, high fashion, earrings, coffee, and other frivolities are scorned! There is an entire chapter devoted to outfitting Rose for the winter, where one fashionable outfit is roundly excoriated, and cast aside in favor of a simple yet well cut frock over warm long underwear! <br /><br />I mean, it's cute and all, and Alcott has a simple, fun style of writing, and I can see that her ideas would have been revolutionary back in the day. But in this day and age, it's like, They really thought she was gonna die because she went ice skating with her male cousins?!<br /><br />The thing that really kept me from enjoying the book though, was the racism. There are a pair of Chinese men in the book who are not only described repeatedly as being beady-eyed and yellow-skinned, and having the "most queer customs and clothing" but are also heartily laughed at by the children for their accents and customs, and go out of their way to give the children gifts and amuse them with their foreign ways. YEEESH.<br /><br />Also, the classist way that Phebe the maid is treated horrified and fascinated me. Rose "adopts" her, which everyone thinks is the most noble and wonderful thing Rose or anyone has ever done. Yet as far as I can tell, this amounts to totally forgetting about her for months at a time, then remembering, feeling bad, being consoled by her uncle, and then giving her a present. Phebe wants to be educated, and Rose remembers this when she comes upon her trying to teach herself, pats her on the head (though Phebe is older) and sets up a play school because she is bored. There is no indication that this school is in session for more than a week. But everyone applauds Rose, and ignores Phebe, as long as she doesn't shirk her duties. Phebe is treated like a very small and backward child, rarely asked what she wants, and Rose is constantly feted for caring about her maid and giving her presents that mostly consist of books and clothes she doesn't want.<br /><br />In short, I can see why this one never became the classic that Little Women did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5976523110314793856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7107038257537069481">Age Appropriate For: All Ages<br/>Best for Ages: 10-18<br/><br/>Some of my favorite memories of my early teen years were the hours that I spent reading Little Women with my sisters. We read the whole book together over a few months, sitting outside in the woods, each with a different project. Alcott always makes me think of those happy times, and makes me feel nostalgic.<br/><br/>My younger sisters have read many more Alcott books then I have, and I got to hear all about their favorites. The book I heard them gush the m</span>
  <span id="freeText7107038257537069481" style="display:none">Age Appropriate For: All Ages<br />Best for Ages: 10-18<br /><br />Some of my favorite memories of my early teen years were the hours that I spent reading Little Women with my sisters. We read the whole book together over a few months, sitting outside in the woods, each with a different project. Alcott always makes me think of those happy times, and makes me feel nostalgic.<br /><br />My younger sisters have read many more Alcott books then I have, and I got to hear all about their favorites. The book I heard them gush the most about was Eight Cousins, so I decided to read it this year. I am so glad I did.<br /><br />While I think that this book can be enjoyed by all ages, it was written for the young. It is a wonderful adventure of a little girl and her cousins, they type of story I would have devoured as a child. There is no-romance, and there are good lessons and interesting characters.<br /><br />Rose was such an endearing character, and I loved how she saw her faults and worked hard to fight against them. I also loved how her Uncle worked so hard to do what was best for her, even if it was against the fashion.<br /><br />The setting, though it was written in the backdrop of the 1800’s, is not very hard to imagine it taking place in our own time. Alcott had a timeless way of writing and a way of capturing the troubles of the old and young alike in a way that makes it never feel old.<br /><br />I highly recommend this to girls who love old books, non-romantic stories, and books that feel real.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7107038257537069481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/796292-kristen">Kristen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Women ages 14 and up</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16127123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8831202749726589895">If you've read any Louisa May Alcott, the general ideas and characters will be familiar. The characters are all very high-minded and very concerned with morality, building character, proper behavior, etc. Being written nearly 150 years ago, some of the ideas on health, class and race relations, and gender roles are very antiquated, and can even seem a bit bigoted. But you have to remember the time in which it was written. The way they describe a Chinese man is particularly interesting.<br/><br/>The basic </span>
  <span id="freeText8831202749726589895" style="display:none">If you've read any Louisa May Alcott, the general ideas and characters will be familiar. The characters are all very high-minded and very concerned with morality, building character, proper behavior, etc. Being written nearly 150 years ago, some of the ideas on health, class and race relations, and gender roles are very antiquated, and can even seem a bit bigoted. But you have to remember the time in which it was written. The way they describe a Chinese man is particularly interesting.<br /><br />The basic plot follows Rose, a girl of means, who is orphaned and goes to live on the "Aunt-Hill" with her many aunts, her seven boy cousins, and her devoted guardian, Uncle Alec. We see her grow, change and learn over the course of an experimental year, in which Uncle Alec has complete charge over her upbringing, with no interference from the aunts. At the end of the year, they are to decide whether it has been a success, and whether Rose should stay with Uncle Alec, or move in with one of her aunts. Does Rose have a good year? Does she stay with Uncle Alec? I'll never tell - you have to read it.<br /><br />Though older books can be a bit difficult to read, due to the different language and writing styles of more than a century ago, I thoroughly enjoyed this book, and plan to read the sequel "A Rose in Bloom" as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8831202749726589895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72166069" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72166069" />
    <a title="Alisha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1790086-alisha"><img alt="Alisha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572145484p2/1790086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72166069?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alisha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alisha" href="/user/show/1790086-alisha">Alisha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1790086-alisha?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1790086-alisha?shelf=happy-books">happy-books</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72166069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15412161280564921712">When I was in my early teens, there was a trifecta of authors that I devoured: Jane Austen, L.M. Montgomery, and Louisa May Alcott. I even made an informational web page about them on Angelfire... those were the days...<br/><br/>Anyway, revisiting a book like Eight Cousins reminds me exactly how I was influenced by these characters. A lot of my world view was shaped by this innocent wholesomeness, exemplified by Rose, the main character. Her ladylike presence automatically inspired people around her (name</span>
  <span id="freeText15412161280564921712" style="display:none">When I was in my early teens, there was a trifecta of authors that I devoured: Jane Austen, L.M. Montgomery, and Louisa May Alcott. I even made an informational web page about them on Angelfire... those were the days...<br /><br />Anyway, revisiting a book like Eight Cousins reminds me exactly how I was influenced by these characters. A lot of my world view was shaped by this innocent wholesomeness, exemplified by Rose, the main character. Her ladylike presence automatically inspired people around her (namely, men) to be their best selves! So much gentility and respect! This impressed me mightily! I subconsciously stored this fascinating social education away, only to find that's not <i>quite</i> how it goes...<br /><br />But while it's a bit out of place in the modern age, and occasionally some would say naive, I'm glad it was part of my youth and wouldn't trade it for anything! <br /><br />In this book, orphaned Rose comes to live with her guardian uncle, and her neighbors are 7 boy cousins and a bunch of other corresponding aunts and uncles!<br /><br />She arrives a tired, droopy little thing who survives on strong coffee, is proud of her tightly cinched little waist, and is about as uneducated as most girls of her class... but Uncle Alec changes all that. By the end of the book she's well on her way to being a truly healthy young girl with high spirits and noble ambitions, who can also make her 7 cousins toe the line.<br /><br />There are some delightful episodes, such as the "freedom suit" her uncle orders for her (the opposite of a corset). And, pay particular attention to the development of Mac, her bookworm cousin... because there's more to come in the next book!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15412161280564921712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2130875603" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2130875603" />
    <a title="Ellen Hamilton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71709113-ellen-hamilton"><img alt="Ellen Hamilton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586799042p2/71709113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2130875603?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen Hamilton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen Hamilton" href="/user/show/71709113-ellen-hamilton">Ellen Hamilton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71709113-ellen-hamilton?shelf=louisa-may-alcott">louisa-may-alcott</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2130875603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1362499475655088214">There are no words to express how lovely this book was to me. I just loved it.<br/><br/>I am wondering though, what exactly was the disagreement between Dr. Alec and Rose's father? Was it that they both loved the same girl, Rose's mother? If so, then I fear that the next book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17533.Rose_in_Bloom__Eight_Cousins___2_' title='Rose in Bloom (Eight Cousins, #2) by Louisa May Alcott' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rose in Bloom</a>, will hurt a bit.<br/><br/>"Fathers and mothers are too absorbed in business and housekeeping to study their children and cherish that sweet and natural confidence, which is a child's surest safeguard, and a parent's subtlest po</span>
  <span id="freeText1362499475655088214" style="display:none">There are no words to express how lovely this book was to me. I just loved it.<br /><br />I am wondering though, what exactly was the disagreement between Dr. Alec and Rose's father? Was it that they both loved the same girl, Rose's mother? If so, then I fear that the next book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17533.Rose_in_Bloom__Eight_Cousins___2_" title="Rose in Bloom (Eight Cousins, #2) by Louisa May Alcott" rel="nofollow noopener">Rose in Bloom</a>, will hurt a bit.<br /><br />"Fathers and mothers are too absorbed in business and housekeeping to study their children and cherish that sweet and natural confidence, which is a child's surest safeguard, and a parent's subtlest power. So the young hearts hide trouble or temptation till the harm is done, and mutual regret comes too late. <br />Happy the boys and girls who tell all things freely to father or mother, sure of pity, help, and pardon; and thrice happy the parents who out of their own experience, and by their own virtues, can teach and uplift the souls for which they are responsible."<br /><br />I cannot help but agree with first part of the quote. As for the second part, I wish that this freedom of confidence existed between my parents and me. It would have helped save me a whole lot of trouble. However, I hope to remember this when I am a parent.<br /><br />I can't talk much more about this book, because.... well, I want to cry over it a little.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1362499475655088214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2639586157" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2639586157" />
    <a title="Gauri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3760321-gauri"><img alt="Gauri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532650487p2/3760321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2639586157?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gauri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gauri" href="/user/show/3760321-gauri">Gauri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140374568" href="/book/show/76624.Eight_Cousins">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who have nothing better to read</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3760321-gauri?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2639586157" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16537470441095867552">Halfway through reading this book, I dismissed it as a saccharine funnel through which Alcott wished to teach children life lessons. After finishing it, I still think this is true, but I realize this book is dated and is more appropriately viewed as a piece of historical work. I mean this in the sense that there are some backwards views that are reflected in this book, but also some surprisingly progressive ones that Alcott cements, which is worth some notice. <br/><br/>There are parts of this book that s</span>
  <span id="freeText16537470441095867552" style="display:none">Halfway through reading this book, I dismissed it as a saccharine funnel through which Alcott wished to teach children life lessons. After finishing it, I still think this is true, but I realize this book is dated and is more appropriately viewed as a piece of historical work. I mean this in the sense that there are some backwards views that are reflected in this book, but also some surprisingly progressive ones that Alcott cements, which is worth some notice. <br /><br />There are parts of this book that stick out sorely: The author wrote only derogatory descriptions of the Chinese characters she included in the book, using the characters as one-dimensional comic entertainers. Characters also continuously mentioned that women had flighty, childlike emotions, and they were not to be taken seriously when emotional. Characters also discussed how women existed to support the men in their lives, or function in a caretaker role for those around them.<br /><br />However, I realized that though backwards prejudices are reflected in this book, this book is at the same time somewhat progressive. Uncle Alec, Rose's guardian, values Rose's opinions and consent to the various things the family encourages her to do. Rose is allowed to speak for herself, and Uncle Alec insists on treating Rose, who is a child, with the consideration and seriousness he would provide adults. Instead of giving Rose medicines and demanding her to rest idly at all times for fear of damaging her constitution, Uncle Alec pushes her to exercise regularly and eat healthily. He forbids her to wear corsets that would damage her body. He defends Rose from her aunts who insist on dressing her in a fashionable but cumbersome getup, and insists on Rose being allowed to choose more comfortable garments that she preferred to wear. He encouraged her to learn important skills like balancing account books, cooking, and clothes-making, so that she would be able to support herself or be independent in some regard. He also taught her subjects like anatomy when she expressed interest in medicine. Uncle Alec praised Rose for her hard work in doing chores and her initiative in educating herself. Work ethic and intelligence were the young girl's most prized values.<br /><br />Rose was treated with respect and was allowed to be free and individualistic -- and I imagine that wasn't very common in those days. These things may be perfectly natural to us now that we barely even make a note of this perspective in media when it's portrayed, but in the 1800's, I doubt girls were actually given that sort of freedom to be their own person. So, I think female authors like Alcott introduced these revolutionary ideas to a whole generation, which adhered to some people and made some social progress.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16537470441095867552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9371491649570294629">If you read Little Women and thought, “Wow, I wonder what other books Louisa May Alcott has written!” just know that I, a Little Woman superfan who has read almost all of Alcott’s books, think all of them are absolutely trash. <br/>I’m sure Alcott was considered a feminist icon back in the Stone Age or whatever, but <i>WOW</i> she sucks at writing women.<br/>She’s unbelievably judgmental when girls want to do anything that involves fashion or beauty. Like, yeah, you don’t need rich dresses or makeup to look pret</span>
  <span id="freeText9371491649570294629" style="display:none">If you read Little Women and thought, “Wow, I wonder what other books Louisa May Alcott has written!” just know that I, a Little Woman superfan who has read almost all of Alcott’s books, think all of them are absolutely trash. <br />I’m sure Alcott was considered a feminist icon back in the Stone Age or whatever, but <i>WOW</i> she sucks at writing women.<br />She’s unbelievably judgmental when girls want to do anything that involves fashion or beauty. Like, yeah, you don’t need rich dresses or makeup to look pretty. But if you want rich dresses and makeup then <b> who the hell is Louisa May to judge you??? </b><br />We get it, you were poor when you grew up so you think that any woman who cares about her appearance is a prostitute. <br />Jesus Christ, the amount of female bashing is literally impossible to read about. The protagonist, who is a 13 year old girl, gets bashed and called silly for getting her ears pierced-something that MANY girls did at that point. Why? Because she’s being <i> vain </i> and <i> no little girl should make herself seem pretty. She should only ever be modest and live in a goddamn nursery as she sews to her death and complains that “this new generation is so conceited!” </i><br />Shut the hell up Alcott. You wrote one good book in your entire life.</span>

<div id="review_186031550" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186031550" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2508518-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/186031550?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/2508518-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186031550" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13891599094512763907">Every so often I get the urge to travel down memory lane and read some of the books that I loved as a child. I went through a phase where I polished off all of the Anne of Green Gables series on my Kindle, and another where I did the Little House books, so I guess it was inevitable that when I next needed to scratch that "childhood period fiction" itch, I'd reach for one of my dearly beloved favorites, Louisa May Alcott. It's interesting to think that many of the authors of beloved children's fi</span>
  <span id="freeText13891599094512763907" style="display:none">Every so often I get the urge to travel down memory lane and read some of the books that I loved as a child. I went through a phase where I polished off all of the Anne of Green Gables series on my Kindle, and another where I did the Little House books, so I guess it was inevitable that when I next needed to scratch that "childhood period fiction" itch, I'd reach for one of my dearly beloved favorites, Louisa May Alcott. It's interesting to think that many of the authors of beloved children's fiction written in or about the 19th century lived themselves very depressing lives(Look up Lucy Maud Montgomery if you don't believe me)that were at odds with the general happiness of the characters they wrote about. LMA is no exception, except in that her characters often reflect some of the own poverty and hardship that she faced in her daily life. They always do so with goodwill and Christian courage, to the point where it gets a little tiresome, but the bits of good writing and humanity that peek through the preaching are delicious enough for me to keep coming back for more. Eight Cousins is one of the lesser read works of LMA, but a favorite of mine. It departs from the usual "poor children maintain good attitudes in the face of struggle" theme by dealing with wealthy children, who maintain good attitudes in the face of struggle. But I don't read it so much for the children as I do for the adults. I think one of the lesser appreciated things about LMA is that while her children might be a little too wholesome for modern audiences to stomach, there's very little wrong with the way she writes adult characters. Consider that the best parts of Little Women are the second half, when the girls are grown, and you'll see what I mean. Eight Cousins features some wonderful grown up characters, and plenty of squeaky clean kids to help me get my childhood fix. Even better I think is "Rose in Bloom" the Eight Cousins sequel where Rose and the boys finally grow up and are therefore permitted enough faults and foibles to make their more saccharine parts go down much easier. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13891599094512763907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer7849310011191192885">Let's face it, Louisa May Alcott wrote a beautiful books for young adult/children. It is a fact.   <span id="freeText7849310011191192885" style="display:none">Let's face it, Louisa May Alcott wrote a beautiful books for young adult/children. It is a fact. Period. ;-)<br /><br />During reading I was thinking all the time that this book should be obligatory for children and also for parents (guardians). There are so many people who read guides for parents and so on. I think they should start with such books like this one. It is so full of wisdom that you can't miss it. And almost all of them are true today too. There weren't computer games or Internet in those times but both then and now children are essentially the same (despite what television or civilisation tell).<br /><br /><i>Fathers and mothers are too absorbed in business and housekeeping to study their children, and cherish that sweet and natural confidence which is a child’s surest safeguard, and a parent’s subtlest power. So the young hearts hide trouble or temptation till the harm is done, and mutual regret comes too late. Happy the boys and girls who tell all things freely to father or mother, sure of pity, help, and pardon; and thrice happy the parents who, out of their own experience, and by their own virtues, can teach and uplift the souls for which they are responsible</i><br /><br />Also, I have had fun reading about plays they have. <br /><br />Of course, my next book will be a sequel: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17533.Rose_in_Bloom__Eight_Cousins___2_" title="Rose in Bloom (Eight Cousins, #2) by Louisa May Alcott" rel="nofollow noopener">Rose in Bloom</a>.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9589670335003636349">This was always my favorite Louisa May Alcott. I listened to Barbara Caruso's narration thanks to an Audible sale.<br/><br/>It was interesting reading this after having recently listened to Heather Ordover's discussion of Little Women at the CraftLit podcast. Essentially Alcott flips the situation of the poor but learning true happiness Little Women and applies it to orphaned, only child Rose who is a considerable heiress from a rich Bostonian family. We spend a year watching her being raised by her bache</span>
  <span id="freeText9589670335003636349" style="display:none">This was always my favorite Louisa May Alcott. I listened to Barbara Caruso's narration thanks to an Audible sale.<br /><br />It was interesting reading this after having recently listened to Heather Ordover's discussion of Little Women at the CraftLit podcast. Essentially Alcott flips the situation of the poor but learning true happiness Little Women and applies it to orphaned, only child Rose who is a considerable heiress from a rich Bostonian family. We spend a year watching her being raised by her bachelor Uncle Alec who applies his medical training and new-fangled ideas (like not wearing corsets). She also gets to be great friends with her seven male cousins who live nearby and that adds to the fun and life lessons in the book.<br /><br />The overall lessons from both books are the same for true happiness, as we would expect, but the way Alcott gets there in this one is charmingly different.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">young girls, and young at heart of all ages</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=absolute-favourites">absolute-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=life-affirming-cosy">life-affirming-cosy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1385703638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5428315021103086220">I first ran across this book over forty years ago, when I was nine or ten. It was and still is one of my "magic carpets"--those books that take you to another time, another place, another situation. I know I'm not the only preadolescent girl who dreamed of a wise, adventuresome Uncle Alec who would turn up and take me away--take me out of school (loved learning, hated school), teach me to swim and ride and boat, shower me with presents, give me lovely comfortable clothes and <i>my own room.</i> As we r</span>
  <span id="freeText5428315021103086220" style="display:none">I first ran across this book over forty years ago, when I was nine or ten. It was and still is one of my "magic carpets"--those books that take you to another time, another place, another situation. I know I'm not the only preadolescent girl who dreamed of a wise, adventuresome Uncle Alec who would turn up and take me away--take me out of school (loved learning, hated school), teach me to swim and ride and boat, shower me with presents, give me lovely comfortable clothes and <i>my own room.</i> As we read we discover that Rose is actually quite the heiress--she lives up on "the hill"--and that hill might just possibly be Beacon, though the name of the town is never given. Due to the illustrations in my book in those days, I thought Rose had black hair, but for once I can forgive a protagonist for being rich, pretty, sweet, AND blonde. <i>My only similarity with Rose is that I too once got near-frostbite on my hands walking home from school in a blizzard with no gloves on a day that started out like spring, as blizzard days often do in the Midwest. My idiot mother, instead of rubbing my hands with snow to jump-start the circulation decided hot water would be better. I too cried out with pain, like Rose--repeatedly--though I didn't develop an interesting and life-threatening case of pneumonia to be nursed through. </i>The boy-cousins who all worship the ground she walks on spoiled me for the guys I went to school with; the Campbell boys are all cute, nice, polite, rich AND intelligent!<br /><br />This is lovely escapist stuff, as much for its author as for its readership--we now know that Alcott's girlhood entailed not-very-warm clothes, "cold water, scant food, and enforced cheerfulness at meals." I always loved the chapter where they visit the Chinese ship best of all. Perhaps that was the seed that led to my fascination with things Asian in general.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5428315021103086220" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer16057951863875883684">This is probably my second favorite Louisa May Alcott book (after Little Women.) At least it was when I was a kid. I remember envying Rose all those boy cousins; it seemed as though it would be so much fun to have so many built in boy playmates. And it’s yet another orphan story – I do have an “orphans” shelf on goodreads as I love books about orphans. I don’t remember this as a depressing or somber tale at all though.<br/><br/>On another note: I pulled out my copy of the book, and I’m very excited as I m</span>
  <span id="freeText16057951863875883684" style="display:none">This is probably my second favorite Louisa May Alcott book (after Little Women.) At least it was when I was a kid. I remember envying Rose all those boy cousins; it seemed as though it would be so much fun to have so many built in boy playmates. And it’s yet another orphan story – I do have an “orphans” shelf on goodreads as I love books about orphans. I don’t remember this as a depressing or somber tale at all though.<br /><br />On another note: I pulled out my copy of the book, and I’m very excited as I must still have my mother’s copy: have one published the year (1927) she was twelve; I read this for the first time when I was a few years younger than that.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16057951863875883684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5910919843564360146" style="display:none">I've loved all the Louisa May Alcott books I've ever read, and this book was no exception. I didn't like it as much as the Little Women series, but I still thought it was great. <div id="review_1740585361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1740585361" />
    <a title="Zoe " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55738895-zoe"><img alt="Zoe " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508765467p2/55738895.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1740585361?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zoe " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zoe " href="/user/show/55738895-zoe">Zoe </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55738895-zoe?shelf=classic-books-i-actually-like">classic-books-i-actually-like</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55738895-zoe?shelf=blog">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1740585361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4340385580078094005">This charming book by Louisa May Alcott is as endearing as always - almost like coming home to a crackly fire and gooey chocolate chip cookies. Alcott will always be one of my favorite authors and I think she does a beautiful job in this book. Timid and weak Rose comes to live at the "Aunt Hill," affectionately nicknamed for its tons of aunts. Rose is an orphan accustomed to staying with dainty, polite girls. Will she survive rambunctious boy cousins and an amiable uncle full of bold ideas? With</span>
  <span id="freeText4340385580078094005" style="display:none">This charming book by Louisa May Alcott is as endearing as always - almost like coming home to a crackly fire and gooey chocolate chip cookies. Alcott will always be one of my favorite authors and I think she does a beautiful job in this book. Timid and weak Rose comes to live at the "Aunt Hill," affectionately nicknamed for its tons of aunts. Rose is an orphan accustomed to staying with dainty, polite girls. Will she survive rambunctious boy cousins and an amiable uncle full of bold ideas? With funny scrapes, sweet stories, and wholesome laughs, you'll fall in love with this dear classic. It's one you'll return to time and time again.<br />I reviewed this book on my <a href="https://www.mypen2paper.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">blog!</a> If you're a writer, a reader, or an eater follow along for sweet eats, regular book reviews, a book club, and advice from published authors.<br />UPDATE:<br />Rose is now an orphan. She never knew her mother and her beloved papa has died. Rose gets sent to live at the Aunt Hill, a group of houses christened for the many aunts who live there. The aunts work themselves into a tizzy over the despondent Rose, doting on her every need. When Uncle Alec, her new guardian, arrives, he's furious! He begins a campaign to turn the meek and mild Rose into a healthy, happy one. That includes no more coffee, chores, boating, and . . . her eight rambunctious cousins. Despite her appalled aunts, Rose joins in the cousins' mischief and hilarity ensues.<br />Eight Cousins and Rose in Bloom aren't books to make you constantly flip through footnotes or a dictionary. (I'm looking at you War and Peace!) They're buoyant novels appropriate for middle grade and adult readers alike. Yet, unlike other children's books, they're genuinely interesting for older readers. My aunt gifted the first one to me a few years ago and I've read it almost seven times since!<br />Laura Linderman said she can never enjoy canon novels. The two reasons she names are that she can't relate to the lifestyle and has to make an effort to try to read them. These books aren't like that. They're enjoyable and, although from a different period, relatable. What more could you want from a series?<br />This book also made it on a different blog list: classic books I actually like! Check out the full post here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.mypen2paper.com/post/classic-books-i-actually-like">https://www.mypen2paper.com/post/clas...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4340385580078094005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_327618451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/327618451" />
    <a title="Tracy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/965766-tracy"><img alt="Tracy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368071029p2/965766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/327618451?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracy" href="/user/show/965766-tracy">Tracy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/11178540-eight-cousins">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/965766-tracy?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/965766-tracy?shelf=teen">teen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer327618451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13007693485412933474">I read this book more than once when I was between the ages of 8 and 12. My recent re-read came about when I realized I could download it for free onto my Kindle. If I were reading it for the first time as an adult, I would probably give it 3 stars, because the moral lessons can be a bit heavy-handed, and the word "pretty" is used 75 times, and the description of the Chinese character Fun See tiptoes too close to being racist, but my nostalgia for the book boosted it to 4 stars. <br/><br/>I was amused to </span>
  <span id="freeText13007693485412933474" style="display:none">I read this book more than once when I was between the ages of 8 and 12. My recent re-read came about when I realized I could download it for free onto my Kindle. If I were reading it for the first time as an adult, I would probably give it 3 stars, because the moral lessons can be a bit heavy-handed, and the word "pretty" is used 75 times, and the description of the Chinese character Fun See tiptoes too close to being racist, but my nostalgia for the book boosted it to 4 stars. <br /><br />I was amused to see that the chapter when they are in the mountains and play charades is just as boring to me now as it was when I was a kid! <br /><br />The book has some modern ideas for being written in the 1870s, but there are other ideas that are alarmingly sexist (reminding you that despite Uncle Alec's forward-thinking it was indeed written in the 1870s!). It is my favorite Alcott book by far, though. As a young reader, I was so completely smitten with the concept of a lonely orphaned girl moving into her Aunts' Boston mansion with seven boy cousins right there in the neighborhood to tease her and love her and admire her and vex her. Oh, how I longed for a clan of boy cousins just like the Campbells! Each boy has a distinct personality, delightful in their own ways, although the 12 year old twin boys, Geordie and Will, don't get much page time. <br /><br />Comments on the Kindle edition: lots of missing punctuation, which made some paragraphs a bit muddled. The character named Annabel Bliss in my print editions here is named Adriana Blish, and Debby the cook is Debby only in the first few chapters, when suddenly her name becomes Dolly for the rest of the story. <br /><br />Oh, and ancient old white-haired Aunt Peace, whom I always pictured as being at least 80 years old, is only 50!!!!!! Yikes! </span>
  <a data-text-id="13007693485412933474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_65459706" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65459706" />
    <a title="Jessika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1166992-jessika"><img alt="Jessika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547989302p2/1166992.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65459706?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessika" href="/user/show/1166992-jessika">Jessika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/6378489-eight-cousins">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Louisa May Alcott or for those looking for something cozy to read</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65459706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7283192254797913514">What a charming, cozy little book--perfect for this time of year to read curled up with a blanket and some hot cocoa! For fans of Louisa May Alcott, this book will not disappoint. It is full to the brim with winsome characters, quaint morals and lessons, and enough heartwarming scenes to leave readers with that "warm, fuzzy feeling" for a while to come. <i>Eight Cousins</i> is not a real page-turner, but I found that I always looked forward to picking it up, even if I was content to put it down after a</span>
  <span id="freeText7283192254797913514" style="display:none">What a charming, cozy little book--perfect for this time of year to read curled up with a blanket and some hot cocoa! For fans of Louisa May Alcott, this book will not disappoint. It is full to the brim with winsome characters, quaint morals and lessons, and enough heartwarming scenes to leave readers with that "warm, fuzzy feeling" for a while to come. <i>Eight Cousins</i> is not a real page-turner, but I found that I always looked forward to picking it up, even if I was content to put it down after a while. Nothing significant happens plot-wise, but it was a joy to watch Rose progress throughout the novel. I was not a fan of Rose at first--I thought she was too spoiled for her own good, but through her uncle's careful guidance and through her interaction with the seven boys, her aunts, and ever-faithful Phebe, Rose grew into one of my favorite characters. My favorite character, though, was Uncle Alec, and he has definitely made his way onto my all-time favorite literary characters list. Alcott writes this novel in such a way that the characters become very dear to the reader by the end, with their quirks and all, and I know that I, for one, am interested in following the fate of this family in the next novel, titled <i>The Rose in Bloom</i>, if I remember correctly. I found Alcott's language to be smooth, elegant, and yet easy to read. It was intelligent and proper without being stuffy, which I particularly enjoyed--this book is worth reading if just for her style of prose. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7283192254797913514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_371615213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/371615213" />
    <a title="Ruth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10711661-ruth"><img alt="Ruth" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/371615213?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruth" href="/user/show/10711661-ruth">Ruth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer371615213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2285833711735803426">I think I just read some brainwashing pamphlet trying to convince everyone that cigars and earrings are evil, all fashion is vanity, and that women were put on the earth to make everything 'comfortable',being too stupid to better themselves with any sort of an education besides the culinary arts and sewing. How did this happen? Did Louisa write this in her old age when she turned senile? Or maybe it was her first novel as a budding author? Either way, this book was a great disappointment--I have</span>
  <span id="freeText2285833711735803426" style="display:none">I think I just read some brainwashing pamphlet trying to convince everyone that cigars and earrings are evil, all fashion is vanity, and that women were put on the earth to make everything 'comfortable',being too stupid to better themselves with any sort of an education besides the culinary arts and sewing. How did this happen? Did Louisa write this in her old age when she turned senile? Or maybe it was her first novel as a budding author? Either way, this book was a great disappointment--I have some recollection of the Little Women series being a tad preachy, but had high expectations for Eight Cousins. Sadly, my high esteem for Alcott has fallen. I still have fond memories of taking part in the lives of the March family and the lives of the young women as they grow up, but let's just say that the series was a one hit wonder. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2285833711735803426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ugFLuTfrXtuQP6jftrOQfU/N9xCUzkiG80HWaHTbOG8Nw+tISRE2CktD2c37+fwAAAnK4Ofr1ECG2oeESt+CNw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MmR2UZkwJL7fA1wZjth0ir4nSZxw2X5+F5Wl0ZVyBPGFptag58pMbwR/LQvDkhj38eN0bAP84rhiDvQ9q3a+qQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1315.Louisa_May_Alcott">About Louisa May Alcott</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1315.Louisa_May_Alcott"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1200326665p3/1315.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1315.Louisa_May_Alcott">Louisa May Alcott
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,647
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5698207830428630483">As <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2765771._A_M__Barnard' title=' A.M. Barnard' rel='nofollow noopener'> A.M. Barnard</a>: <br/>Behind a Mask, or a Woman's Power (1866)<br/>The Abbot's Ghost, or Maurice Treherne's Temptation (1867)<br/>A Long Fatal Love Chase (1866 – first published 1995)<br/>First published anonymously:<br/>A Modern Mephistopheles (1877)<br/><br/>Louisa May Alcott was born in Germantown, Pennsylvania on November 29, 1832. She and her three sisters, Anna, Elizabeth and May were educated by their father, philosopher/ t</span>
  <span id="freeText5698207830428630483" style="display:none">As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2765771._A_M__Barnard" title=" A.M. Barnard" rel="nofollow noopener"> A.M. Barnard</a>: <br />Behind a Mask, or a Woman's Power (1866)<br />The Abbot's Ghost, or Maurice Treherne's Temptation (1867)<br />A Long Fatal Love Chase (1866 – first published 1995)<br />First published anonymously:<br />A Modern Mephistopheles (1877)<br /><br />Louisa May Alcott was born in Germantown, Pennsylvania on November 29, 1832. She and her three sisters, Anna, Elizabeth and May were educated by their father, philosopher/ teacher, Bronson Alcott and raised on the practical Christianity of their mother, Abigail May.<br /><br />Louisa spent her childhood in Boston and in Concord, Massachusetts, where her days were enlightened by visits to Ralph Waldo Emerson’s library, excursions into nature with Henry David Thoreau and theatricals in the barn at Hillside (now Hawthorne’s "Wayside").<br /><br />Like her character, Jo March in Little Women, young Louisa was a tomboy: "No boy could be my friend till I had beaten him in a race," she claimed, " and no girl if she refused to climb trees, leap fences...."<br /><br />For Louisa, writing was an early passion. She had a rich imagination and often her stories became melodramas that she and her sisters would act out for friends. Louisa preferred to play the "lurid" parts in these plays, "the villains, ghosts, bandits, and disdainful queens."<br /><br />At age 15, troubled by the poverty that plagued her family, she vowed: "I will do something by and by. Don’t care what, teach, sew, act, write, anything to help the family; and I’ll be rich and famous and happy before I die, see if I won’t!"<br /><br />Confronting a society that offered little opportunity to women seeking employment, Louisa determined "...I will make a battering-ram of my head and make my way through this rough and tumble world." Whether as a teacher, seamstress, governess, or household servant, for many years Louisa did any work she could find.<br /><br />Louisa’s career as an author began with poetry and short stories that appeared in popular magazines. In 1854, when she was 22, her first book Flower Fables was published. A milestone along her literary path was Hospital Sketches (1863) based on the letters she had written home from her post as a nurse in Washington, DC as a nurse during the Civil War.<br /><br />When Louisa was 35 years old, her publisher Thomas Niles in Boston asked her to write "a book for girls." Little Women was written at Orchard House from May to July 1868. The novel is based on Louisa and her sisters’ coming of age and is set in Civil War New England. Jo March was the first American juvenile heroine to act from her own individuality; a living, breathing person rather than the idealized stereotype then prevalent in children’s fiction.<br /><br />In all, Louisa published over 30 books and collections of stories. She died on March 6, 1888, only two days after her father, and is buried in Sleepy Hollow Cemetery in Concord.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5698207830428630483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
